## Chapter: Chapter 11: Thermodynamics of Aqueous Solutions

### Introduction

The study of thermodynamics is a fundamental aspect of geochemistry, providing a framework for understanding the energy changes and equilibrium conditions that govern the behavior of chemical species in geological systems. In this chapter, we will delve into the specific realm of aqueous solutions, exploring the unique thermodynamic properties and behaviors that these systems exhibit.

Aqueous solutions are ubiquitous in geology, playing a crucial role in processes such as weathering, sedimentation, and mineral formation. The thermodynamics of these solutions are governed by the principles of chemical equilibrium, enthalpy, and entropy, among others. Understanding these principles is essential for predicting the behavior of aqueous solutions under different conditions, and for interpreting the geochemical data that these solutions yield.

We will begin this chapter by introducing the basic concepts of aqueous solutions, including the definitions of terms such as "activity" and "chemical potential". We will then explore the thermodynamic properties of these solutions, including their enthalpy and entropy, and how these properties influence the behavior of aqueous solutions. We will also discuss the concept of chemical equilibrium in aqueous solutions, and how it is influenced by factors such as temperature and pressure.

Finally, we will apply these concepts to real-world geochemical problems, demonstrating how the principles of thermodynamics can be used to interpret geochemical data and make predictions about the behavior of aqueous solutions in geological systems.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive introduction to the thermodynamics of aqueous solutions, equipping readers with the knowledge and tools necessary to understand and predict the behavior of these systems. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field of geochemistry, we hope that this chapter will serve as a valuable resource in your studies and work.




#### 10.2b Properties of Non-ideal Solid Solutions

Non-ideal solid solutions exhibit a range of properties that deviate from those of ideal solutions. These properties are primarily due to the interactions between the different types of atoms or ions in the solution, which can be attractive or repulsive. 

##### Entropy of Solution

The entropy of a solution is a measure of the disorder or randomness in the arrangement of the atoms or ions in the solution. In non-ideal solutions, the entropy is higher than in ideal solutions, due to the interactions between the different types of atoms or ions. This increased entropy can lead to a higher melting point and hardness in non-ideal solid solutions compared to ideal solutions.

##### Thermodynamic Properties

The thermodynamic properties of non-ideal solid solutions can be calculated from the thermodynamic properties of the constituent elements. However, the calculations are more complex due to the interactions between the different types of atoms or ions. For example, the Gibbs free energy of a non-ideal solution can be expressed as:

$$
G = \sum_{i} x_i \Delta G_i + \sum_{i} \sum_{j} x_i x_j \Delta G_{ij}
$$

where $x_i$ and $x_j$ are the mole fractions of the components $i$ and $j$, and $\Delta G_i$ and $\Delta G_{ij}$ are the Gibbs free energy changes of component $i$ and the interaction between components $i$ and $j$, respectively.

##### Physical Properties

The physical properties of non-ideal solid solutions, such as their melting point and hardness, can also be predicted from the properties of the constituent elements. However, the predictions are less accurate than for ideal solutions, due to the interactions between the different types of atoms or ions. For example, the melting point of a non-ideal solid solution can be expressed as:

$$
T_m = \sum_{i} x_i T_{m,i} + \sum_{i} \sum_{j} x_i x_j T_{m,ij}
$$

where $T_{m,i}$ and $T_{m,ij}$ are the melting points of component $i$ and the interaction between components $i$ and $j$, respectively.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the thermodynamics of non-ideal solid solutions, focusing on the concept of excess Gibbs free energy and its role in understanding the properties of these solutions.

#### 10.2c Applications of Non-ideal Solid Solutions

Non-ideal solid solutions have a wide range of applications in various fields, including materials science, chemistry, and geology. The unique properties of non-ideal solutions, such as their higher entropy and complex thermodynamic and physical properties, make them particularly useful in these applications.

##### Materials Science

In materials science, non-ideal solid solutions are often used to create alloys with unique properties. For example, the addition of a small amount of a second metal to a first metal can significantly increase the hardness of the alloy, a phenomenon known as solid solution strengthening. This is due to the interactions between the different types of atoms or ions in the solution, which can disrupt the regular arrangement of atoms in the metal lattice and increase the entropy of the solution.

Non-ideal solid solutions are also used in the development of new materials with tailored properties. By carefully controlling the composition and conditions of the solution, it is possible to create materials with specific properties, such as high melting points, high hardness, or specific electrical or magnetic properties.

##### Chemistry

In chemistry, non-ideal solid solutions are used in a variety of reactions, including precipitation reactions, redox reactions, and acid-base reactions. The interactions between the different types of atoms or ions in the solution can affect the rate and outcome of these reactions, making non-ideal solutions a valuable tool in chemical synthesis.

Non-ideal solid solutions are also used in the study of chemical equilibria. The Gibbs free energy expression for non-ideal solutions, as shown in the previous section, allows for the calculation of the equilibrium constant for reactions in non-ideal solutions. This is particularly useful in systems where the concentration of one or more species is not constant, such as in biological systems.

##### Geology

In geology, non-ideal solid solutions are used to study the properties of minerals and rocks. The interactions between different types of atoms or ions in a mineral can affect its physical and chemical properties, such as its hardness, melting point, and reactivity. By studying these interactions, geologists can gain insights into the formation and evolution of minerals and rocks.

Non-ideal solid solutions are also used in the development of new materials for geotechnical applications, such as cement and concrete. By carefully controlling the composition and conditions of the solution, it is possible to create materials with specific properties, such as high strength, durability, or resistance to corrosion.

In conclusion, non-ideal solid solutions have a wide range of applications in various fields, and their unique properties make them a valuable tool in the study and development of new materials and substances.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of thermodynamics of solid solutions. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern the behavior of solid solutions, and how these principles can be applied to understand and predict the properties of geological systems. 

We have learned that solid solutions are not ideal, and their properties deviate from those of the ideal solutions. These deviations are due to the interactions between the different types of atoms or ions in the solution, which can be attractive or repulsive. 

We have also seen how the thermodynamic properties of solid solutions, such as the Gibbs free energy, can be used to predict the stability of these solutions. We have learned that a negative Gibbs free energy change indicates a spontaneous process, while a positive Gibbs free energy change indicates a non-spontaneous process.

Finally, we have discussed the concept of chemical potential, which is a key concept in thermodynamics. We have seen how the chemical potential can be used to understand the behavior of solid solutions, and how it can be used to predict the direction of spontaneous processes.

In conclusion, the thermodynamics of solid solutions is a complex and fascinating field that has wide-ranging applications in geology. By understanding the principles and concepts discussed in this chapter, we can gain a deeper understanding of the behavior of geological systems, and make more accurate predictions about their future behavior.

A solid solution is formed when 50% of a metal A is replaced by a metal B. If the Gibbs free energy of formation of the solution is -20 kJ/mol, calculate the chemical potential of the solution at 25C.

#### Exercise 3
A solid solution is formed when 25% of a metal A is replaced by a metal B. If the enthalpy of solution is -5 kJ/mol and the entropy of solution is 80 J/molK, calculate the Gibbs free energy of formation of the solution at 25C.

#### Exercise 4
A solid solution is formed when 30% of a metal A is replaced by a metal B. If the chemical potential of the solution is -15 kJ/mol, calculate the Gibbs free energy of formation of the solution at 25C.

#### Exercise 5
A solid solution is formed when 40% of a metal A is replaced by a metal B. If the enthalpy of solution is -7 kJ/mol and the entropy of solution is 90 J/molK, calculate the Gibbs free energy of formation of the solution at 25C.

## Chapter: Chapter 11: Thermodynamics of Liquid Solutions

### Introduction

In the realm of geology, understanding the properties and behavior of liquid solutions is of paramount importance. This chapter, "Thermodynamics of Liquid Solutions," delves into the fundamental principles of thermodynamics as they apply to liquid solutions, providing a comprehensive understanding of the subject matter.

Liquid solutions, in the context of geology, refer to the dissolution of minerals in a liquid medium, such as water. The study of these solutions is crucial in various fields, including hydrogeology, environmental science, and petrology. The thermodynamics of these solutions can provide insights into the solubility of minerals, the stability of solutions, and the direction of spontaneous processes.

The chapter will explore the concept of chemical potential, a key concept in thermodynamics, and how it applies to liquid solutions. It will also delve into the Gibbs free energy, a measure of the maximum reversible work that a system can perform at constant temperature and pressure. The Gibbs free energy is a critical concept in understanding the spontaneity of processes in liquid solutions.

Furthermore, the chapter will discuss the concept of chemical equilibrium and how it is influenced by the Gibbs free energy. This will provide a deeper understanding of the behavior of liquid solutions, including their ability to resist changes from external influences.

In essence, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the thermodynamics of liquid solutions, equipping geologists with the necessary tools to predict and understand the behavior of liquid solutions in various geological contexts.
